SynapseFlow first analyzes source code to construct Structural Flow Graphs and extract coherent Function Triplets. It then synthesizes harnesses via a decomposed four-stage process governed by a staged rollback algorithm to ensure correctness. We evaluated SynapseFlow on 25 real-world open-source software projects. The experimental results indicate that SynapseFlow outperforms state-of-the-art tools (OSS-Fuzz-Gen, CKGFuzzer, PromeFuzz), achieving 3.07$\times$, 1.71$\times$, and 4.26$\times$ higher branch coverage, and 1.77$\times$, 1.51$\times$, and 1.36$\times$ higher bug detection rates, respectively. Then, SPELLSMITH leverages the Description property of the protocol to embed behavioral guidance (Description Enhancement Module) and exploits LLMs' self-reflection capabilities (Self-Reflection Module) to iteratively evaluate and refine outputs. By strengthening LLM internal decision-making, SPELLSMITH provides an active and unified mitigation strategy that generalizes across multiple vulnerabilities, reducing reliance on context-specific code-level fixes. ### [238] [When Agents Remember Too Much: Memory Poisoning Attacks on Large Language Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.06595)
*George Torres,Sharad Shrestha,Satyajayant Misra*

Main category: cs.CR

TL;DR: Personal AI agents using large language models with long-term memory can be attacked via a new method, GhostWriter, which poisons their memory stores. A proposed defense mechanism significantly reduces the attack's effectiveness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address emerging security vulnerabilities in personal AI agents augmented with memory systems, particularly when interacting with sensitive and untrusted sources.

Method: GhostWriter, a novel two-phase attack vector, is introduced. The phases are injection (hiding an attack payload in memory) and activation (retrieving the poisoned memory). A new defense mechanism, AM-Sentry, applies memory-saving policies and retrieval screening.

Result: GhostWriter shows a 98% injection rate and a 60% activation rate on state-of-the-art agents, while AM-Sentry significantly reduces these rates without harming performance.

Conclusion: GhostWriter highlights security risks in memory subsystems of personal AI agents. AM-Sentry offers an effective mitigation strategy, enhancing memory governance and security without degrading functionality.

Abstract: Personal AI agents powered by large language models can reason and act using available tools to access emails, manage calendars, and push code to remote repositories, all with minimal oversight. When augmented with long-term memory, an agent can recall specific details relevant to the current task, reducing the need for large context windows. Currently, long-term memory agents tend to fall into two distinct domains: conversational and action-planning agents. Personal assistant agents sit at the convergence of these two domains and handle sensitive information while interacting with untrusted information sources, creating previously unaccounted security vulnerabilities. In this work, we introduce the novel attack vector, GhostWriter, which exploits current memory subsystems in tool-using personal agents to poison their memory store. GhostWriter operates in two phases: injection, where an adversary sends a hidden attack payload to the target agent; and activation, in which the poisoned memory is retrieved. We show that GhostWriter achieves near-universal injection rates of approximately 98% and a high average activation rate of approximately 60% against state-of-the-art agents. This attack is possible due to the lack of security-focused memory governance. In response, we propose Agentic Memory Sentry (AM-Sentry), which leverages two mitigation techniques: a memory-saving policy and a memory-retrieval screen. Our experiments show that AM-Sentry dramatically reduces GhostWriter's success rate while preserving agent utility.

</details>

### [241] [Beyond Attack-Success Rate: Action-Graded Severity Scale for Tool-Using AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07474)
*Harry Owiredu-Ashley*

Main category: cs.CR

TL;DR: This paper critiques binary attack-success metrics in agentic red-teaming, proposing a harm severity scale (L0 to L6) to provide nuanced insights into harmful outcomes of agent actions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Binary metrics in agentic red-teaming overlook the severity of harmful actions, limiting defenders’ capacity to assess the real impact of threats.

Method: The authors introduce a seven-level graded harm rubric to evaluate agent actions based on reversibility, scope-crossing, and privilege escalation, using deterministic oracles and language-model judges for scoring.

Result: Severity grading revealed overlooked harmful cases in existing defenses, and demonstrated high agreement between human judges and deterministic oracle (Krippendorff’s alpha = 0.91).

Conclusion: The paper presents a reusable severity instrument for analyzing agent harm from existing red-team logs, emphasizing its utility in understanding attack impacts more comprehensively than binary metrics.

Abstract: Agentic red-teaming benchmarks report whether an injected agent was compromised as a single bit: the attack succeeded, or it did not. We argue that this binary attack-success rate discards the information a defender most needs, namely how harmful the resulting action was. We introduce an action-graded harm rubric that scores an agent's tool-call trajectory on a seven-level ordinal scale (L0 to L6) according to whether the executed action was reversible, whether it crossed scope to reach another party, and whether it expanded privilege. We compute the scale two ways: a deterministic oracle that reads the trajectory and the attacker's stated goal, and a panel of three frontier language-model judges that read a tag-free account of the same trajectory. Across four victim models and two defenses on the AgentDojo workspace suite, severity grading exposes three cases the binary metric hides, including a defense that reports a zero attack-success rate while still permitting an externally visible cross-scope leak through an unfiltered tool. The judge panel reproduces the oracle with high ordinal agreement (Krippendorff's alpha = 0.91) but shares systematic blind spots that we characterize, most notably a failure to recognize escalation chains. Unlike prior work that provides harm taxonomies, harmful-task completion tests, execution-level safety benchmarks, or severity-aware simulation, our contribution is a reusable, trace-grounded severity instrument applied to the actual actions recorded in existing red-team logs. All code, prompts, and per-episode logs are released.

</details>


### [242] [PRoVeFL: Private Robust and Verifiable Aggregation in Federated Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.06612)
*Harsh Kasyap,Anil Kumar Pradhan,Ugur Ilker Atmaca,Graham Cormode,Carsten Maple*

Main category: cs.CR

TL;DR: PRoVeFL proposes a new federated learning framework achieving privacy, Byzantine robustness, and verifiable aggregation efficiently, improving upon prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current FL methods are vulnerable to server-side inference and client-side attacks, while existing resilient protocols trade-off privacy, integrity, and practicality.

Method: PRoVeFL employs multi-key fully homomorphic encryption with encrypted shares distributed among servers, enabling hybrid computations with privacy constraints and robust verifiable aggregation.

Result: PRoVeFL demonstrates better scalability, supports advanced aggregation algorithms, and outperforms similar frameworks like Prio and ELSA by up to 100x in runtime.

Conclusion: The framework balances privacy, integrity, and verifiability efficiently, advancing FL's scalability and security.

Abstract: Federated Learning (FL) enables multiple clients to collaboratively train machine learning models while retaining data locality, thereby enhancing user privacy. However, traditional FL frameworks rely on a centralized aggregation server and assume honest-but-curious clients, making them susceptible to both server-side inference and client-side poisoning attacks. Although recent work has explored secure and Byzantine-resilient FL protocols, they face a fundamental trade-off among privacy, integrity, and verifiability, and incur substantial computational and communication overhead due to the heavy use of cryptographic primitives. In this work, we propose PRoVeFL-a novel, modular FL framework that is Privacy-preserving, Byzantine-Robust, and ensures Verifiable aggregation. PRoVeFL employs multiple servers leveraging multi-key fully homomorphic encryption. Each client encrypts its local model updates and distributes encrypted shares to all servers. This design enables a hybrid computation model in which ciphertext operations are carefully offloaded to the plaintext domain under strict privacy constraints to efficiently evaluate complex statistical aggregation rules. PRoVeFL is compatible with a wide range of state-of-the-art Byzantine-robust aggregation algorithms (e.g., Krum, Trimmed Mean, FLTrust, norm clipping, MESAS, and more) and further enhances them with verifiability mechanisms that require minimal trust in at least one honest server. We evaluate it across different settings and demonstrate its scalability with varying numbers of parameters and participants. PRoVeFL improves runtime over the prior works, Prio and ELSA, based on distributed trust with comparable security guarantees, up to 100x and 10x, respectively.

Every multiple double arithmetical operation requires renormalization, which involves branching, for which tensor cores are unsuited.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper seeks to address the challenge of leveraging information from multiple source domains to improve classification in a target domain while accounting for shared and domain-specific variations.

Method: The authors employ spiked covariance models to derive deterministic limits, identify oracle transfer weights, develop plug-in estimators, and propose a correction for target-domain sample imbalance.

Result: The study quantifies how shared signals, domain-specific variations, and other structural factors influence transfer learning performance, and achieves consistent estimators and bias corrections.

Conclusion: The work provides theoretical foundations and practical tools for effective transfer learning in high-dimensional linear discriminant analysis, accounting for domain-specific heterogeneities and imbalances.

Abstract: This paper studies transfer learning for linear discriminant analysis in high-dimensional two-class classification. We consider one target domain and several source domains, where the mean difference in each domain is decomposed into a deterministic common component and a domain-specific random deviation. The common component represents a shared classification signal across domains, while the random deviation captures domain-specific heterogeneity. Under spiked covariance models, we derive deterministic limits for the target-domain Gaussian-calibrated error of weighted transfer classifiers under both homogeneous and heterogeneous covariance settings. These limits quantify the effects of the shared signal, domain-specific variation, dimension-to-sample-size ratios, and spike structures on transfer performance. They further lead to oracle transfer weights and consistent data-driven plug-in estimators. We also characterize the intercept bias induced by unbalanced target-domain class sample sizes and provide an asymptotically optimal correction.

Main category: stat.ME

TL;DR: The paper develops a post-selection assessment framework for partially exploratory factor analysis using Bayesian variable selection techniques. It introduces a scale-free gain rule for determining factor numbers effectively.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address the challenge of weakly specified loading structures and factor numbers in partially exploratory factor analysis, improving dimensionality recovery and model fit accuracy.

Method: The paper uses a regularized variational approximation approach with spike and slab priors for Bayesian variable selection. It converts converged solutions into covariance models via hard and soft selection methods, evaluates fit diagnostics, and employs a gain rule for factor number determination.

Result: Simulations demonstrate index success in tracking loading recovery and identifying under-factoring. The proposed gain rule effectively recovers true dimensionality with ELBO being robust, and application to a 100-item inventory shows improved model fit over confirmatory models.

Conclusion: The proposed methods for post-selection assessment and factor determination successfully address weaknesses in exploratory factor analysis, showing promise in recovering true dimensional structures and enhancing model fit across diverse applications.

Abstract: In partially exploratory factor analysis (PEFA), the loading structure and factor numbers are weakly specified. The regularized variational approximation for partially confirmatory factor analysis (PCFA VA) recovers this structure via Bayesian variable selection, using spike and slab priors to assign inclusion probabilities to unspecified loadings. This research introduces a post selection assessment framework for this approach. We convert converged solutions into covariance models using either hard selection (thresholding probabilities into a sparse pattern) or soft selection (retaining them as weights for effective parameter counts). We derive the resulting degrees of freedom, absolute fit diagnostics (RMSEA, SRMR, CFI, TLI), and relative criteria (AIC, BIC, ELBO). To determine factor numbers, we propose a scale free gain rule with a sustained drop guard. Simulations show absolute indices successfully track loading recovery and flag under factoring. While raw criteria over factor, our gain rule accurately recovers true dimensionality, with the ELBO variant proving most robust. Finally, a 100 item PID 5 example demonstrates that our model fits better than a confirmatory 25 facet model and concordantly recovers major structures across disjoint specifications.

</details>

### [248] [Quantum Software Engineering in Practice: FPGA and AI Integration for Quantum Certification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07597)
*Marcos Guillermo Lammers,José Manuel Suárez,Adrián Pousa,Luis Mariano Bibbó,Alejandro Fernández*

Main category: quant-ph

TL;DR: This paper introduces QAccCert, a hybrid certification framework for verifying quantum devices, utilizing QSE principles, FPGAs, AI, and LLM-guided optimization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper aims to address the pressing need for reliable certification of quantum devices, ensuring they produce valid entangled states despite hardware imperfections, noise, and decoherence.

Method: The authors developed QAccCert, an entanglement certification framework based on CHSH inequality violation, leveraging Qiskit AerSimulator, FPGAs, AI, and LLM-guided parameter optimization.

Result: The framework achieved 99.94% of the theoretical maximum of $2\sqrt{2}$ in ideal quantum simulations, showcasing efficient parameter space exploration compared to random search.

Conclusion: This study demonstrates the potential of QSE approaches and hybrid technology integration in achieving scalable quantum certification, serving as a practical case study for future implementation on real NISQ hardware.

Abstract: The emergence of Quantum Software Engineering (QSE) responds to the need for systematic, disciplined, and quantifiable approaches to the development, operation, and maintenance of quantum software. Within this context, quantum computer certification represents a significant challenge: verifying that quantum devices produce valid entangled states despite hardware imperfections, noise, and decoherence. This paper presents QAccCert, a hybrid certification framework developed following QSE principles, demonstrating how heterogeneous technologies like FPGAs and Artificial Intelligence can be integrated for quantum processing. The framework implements entanglement certification through CHSH inequality violation in ideal quantum simulations using Qiskit AerSimulator. Through LLM-guided optimization, the system achieves 99.94% of the theoretical maximum of $2\sqrt{2}$, evidencing more efficient parameter space exploration than random search. These simulated results illustrate how QSE methodologies, combined with strategic technology interconnection, can be applied for practical and scalable quantum certification on real NISQ hardware in future work. This study provides a concrete case study of systematic quantum software development.

</details>

### [249] [Local large deviations for linear-region growth in random piecewise-linear networks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07014)
*Recep Özkan,Christian Hirsch*

Main category: math.PR

TL;DR: The paper investigates affine growth in deep piecewise-linear networks, focusing on the number of affine regions after layers and their exponential bounds. Key contributions include analyses of submultiplicative pressures and certified lower bounds via finite-state defect processes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand the growth dynamics of affine regions in deep neural networks, specifically how these regions scale and can be bounded after introducing random perturbations.

Method: The authors study a model based on i.i.d. perturbations of a symmetric tent map, analyze submultiplicative pressures, and introduce finite-state defect processes to establish constructions and bounds.

Result: The paper derives exponential bounds for the logarithmic growth rate of affine regions (upper and lower tails) and links these growth properties to submultiplicative pressure and bridge-word-based analysis.

Conclusion: The results provide mathematical insights into the complexity and growth patterns of affine regions in piecewise-linear networks, with implications for their structural properties under random perturbations.

Abstract: We study a random compositional model for the growth of affine regions in deep piecewise-linear networks. The model is generated by i.i.d.\ perturbations of the symmetric height-one tent map, and the main observable is the number \(N_n\) of affine pieces after \(n\) layers. We prove the existence of a submultiplicative pressure for \(N_n\), yielding exponential upper bounds for both tails of \(n^{-1}\log N_n\). The same argument applies to abstract submultiplicative complexity observables and gives higher-dimensional extensions for convex-polytopal affine-cover counts and worst-line affine-piece counts. Since the true branch count has no matching supermultiplicative inequality, lower bounds require a separate certified construction. We introduce a finite-state defect process that records branches whose future splitting can be guaranteed, and use bridge words to obtain constructive upper-tail lower bounds. In a uniformly favorable small-noise regime, this process is governed by a companion matrix whose Perron root tends to \(2\), implying eventual exclusion of lower tails below \(\log 2-ξ\).

</details>

### [250] [Reconfigurable Radiology Labels Without Relabeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.06597)
*Jean-Benoit Delbrouck,Dave Van Veen,Akash Pattnaik,Kalina Slavkova,Javid Abderezaei,Harris Bergman,Khan Siddiqui*

Main category: eess.IV

TL;DR: The paper presents a pipeline to convert free-text chest radiograph (CXR) reports into multi-label matrices, enabling faster and cost-effective reconfiguration of label schemas without relabeling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Public CXR datasets typically have limited label schemas, which fail to represent the full range of findings documented in free-text radiology reports. The authors aim to design a system that enables flexible and efficient adaptation of labels for varying tasks and requirements.

Method: The authors develop a pipeline that processes free-text radiology reports into multi-label matrices. Labels can be reconfigured by simply modifying a dictionary, avoiding the need for time-consuming and costly relabeling passes.

Result: Their pipeline processes MIMIC-CXR (223K reports) in 196 seconds for reconfigurations without API costs, a significant improvement over methods costing $6,600. It extends label coverage to findings outside the standard CheXpert-14 scheme while maintaining comparable performance for shared targets.

Conclusion: Structuring radiology reports into editable multi-label matrices streamlines label schema reconfiguration, allowing flexible and efficient adaptation to new tasks without the need for corpus relabeling.

Abstract: Public chest-radiograph (CXR) datasets are typically released with small, fixed label schemas such as CheXpert-14. However, the underlying free-text reports describe far more findings -- and which findings matter depends on the task, site, and reader. We release a pipeline that converts free-text reports into multi-label matrices and then reconfigures the label schema through dictionary edits rather than new inference passes, i.e., without relabeling the corpus. After this one-time pass, reconfiguring MIMIC-CXR (223K reports) from cached annotations takes 196 seconds with no API cost, compared to \$6.6K for an equivalent relabeling pass with Claude Opus 4.7. Using a 58-label taxonomy, we show that 43\% of CXR studies contain at least one finding outside CheXpert-14. Image probes trained on these labels match CheXpert-14 probes on shared targets while also reaching 0.78 AUROC on expert-reviewed long-tail labels that CheXpert-14 cannot represent. These results suggest a different unit of work for radiology labeling: once reports are structured, the label schema becomes a configuration to edit, not a corpus to relabel.

</details>


### [251] [Non-contact, Real-time, Heart-rate Measurement using Image Processing with Commodity Cameras and AI Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.06598)
*Kelly Li,Fulu Li*

Main category: eess.IV

TL;DR: This paper introduces a system for real-time, non-contact heart rate measurement using image processing and commodity cameras.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address the limitation of traditional contact-based heart rate measurement methods, particularly for elderly health monitoring.

Method: Developed a heart rate computation system leveraging image processing, involving four steps: frame rate detection, face detection via deep learning, signal de-noising through time sliding window, and heart rate calculation from periodic signals.

Result: Prototypes were tested against Apple Watch results for accuracy, and mean differences were analyzed for consistency.

Conclusion: The system shows promise for non-contact heart rate monitoring and will undergo further refinement and optimization.

Abstract: Heart rate measurement is one of the key requirements for real-time health monitoring, in particular for health caring of elderly people. Traditional heart rate measurement relies on contact sensing mechanisms such as some heart rate measurement devices at medical hospitals or some wearable devices with embedded sensors such as Apple Watch, etc. In this paper, we develop a system for non-contact, real-time, heart rate measurement using image processing with commodity cameras such as an embedded camera on a laptop, where we use an innovative algorithm to capture the relevant signals for the computation of heart rate in a time series in real life environments. The presented heart rate computation (HRC) process is composed with four major steps: (a) identify frames per second of the camera in use, i.e., 30 frames per second for a given camera, (b) face detection (FD) with shape predictor of 68 face landmarks using deep learning (DL) method, (c) time sliding window (TSW) algorithm to de-noise the signal by smoothing out the noise, and (d) compute heart rate based on identified signal periodicity. We test and analyze the developed prototypes against heart rate results by Apple Watch and check the difference range in multiple rounds and compute the mean of the difference for the measurement values of the heart rate of the same person at the same time. We will do further tuning and optimization of the present methods and deploy the system as a personal AI agent [6] for health monitoring as our future directions.

</details>

### [252] [Can Reinforcement Learning Efficiently Discover Price Manipulation?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.06121)
*Ioanna-Yvonni Tsaknaki,Andrea Macrì,Fabrizio Lillo*

Main category: q-fin.TR

TL;DR: This paper explores whether a model-free reinforcement learning (RL) agent can outperform traditional model-based techniques in identifying and exploiting price manipulation opportunities. The study employs both RL and model-based approaches in a simulated financial market and highlights RL's advantages in intermediate volatility scenarios.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper aims to determine the effectiveness of RL agents in exploiting and identifying price manipulation strategies compared to traditional model-based methods, particularly under varying levels of market volatility.

Method: The authors simulate a single-asset market using the Almgren-Chriss framework, compare Sequential Least Squares Quadratic Programming for optimal strategies with two learning approaches: model-based estimation and Deep Deterministic Policy Gradient (model-free RL).

Result: For intermediate volatility, the RL agent identified profitable manipulative strategies better than the model-based procedure, especially when model-based estimates suffered from sampling errors. However, RL struggled in high-volatility markets and underperformed in low-volatility scenarios.

Conclusion: While RL demonstrates significant potential in tackling complex control problems like financial market manipulation, its effectiveness varies with market conditions. Safeguards are necessary when deploying RL in financial markets to mitigate associated risks.

Abstract: In this paper, we investigate whether a model-free RL agent can identify and exploit price manipulation opportunities more effectively than a traditional model-based approach that assumes correct specification of the data-generating process but relies on noisy parameter estimates. We consider a single-asset market in which prices evolve according to an Almgren-Chriss framework with non-linear permanent impact and linear temporary impact. We first establish the existence of price-manipulative strategies in discrete time and compute the optimal benchmark strategy using Sequential Least Squares Quadratic Programming under full information. We then compare two finite-sample learning approaches: a model-based procedure that estimates impact parameters from simulated execution data and an agnostic RL approach based on Deep Deterministic Policy Gradient, trained directly on the same amount of data. For intermediate volatility, the RL agent successfully discovers profitable manipulative strategies without explicit knowledge of the underlying model, even when training data are quite limited. More importantly, RL consistently outperforms the model-based approach when parameter estimates are affected by sampling error, despite the latter benefiting from the correct model specification. For large volatility, all methods are unable to identify manipulation opportunities, while for small volatility, the model based approach outperforms RL. These findings highlight both the effectiveness of RL in complex control problems and the risks associated with deploying learning algorithms in financial markets without appropriate safeguards.

</details>
